  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_9d18b56d-9cba-4fcd-9e32-53ddccd78400'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };

<span id="freeTextContainer547698777162327745">There came an elastic aftershock of creaks and groans and then, softly softly, a chinking shower of rubbled cement. Leaves calmed and trunks stood serene. Where, not a minute before, there had been a suburb, there was now only woodland standing amid ruins…<br/><br/>There is no warning.   <span id="freeText547698777162327745" style="display:none">There came an elastic aftershock of creaks and groans and then, softly softly, a chinking shower of rubbled cement. Leaves calmed and trunks stood serene. Where, not a minute before, there had been a suburb, there was now only woodland standing amid ruins…<br /><br />There is no warning. No chance to prepare.<br /><br />They arrive in the night: thundering up through the ground, transforming streets and towns into shadowy forest. Buildings are destroyed. Broken bodies, still wrapped in tattered bed linen, hang among the twitching leaves.<br /><br />Adrien Thomas has never been much of a hero. But when he realises that no help is coming, he ventures out into this unrecognisable world. Michelle, his wife, is across the sea in Ireland and he has no way of knowing whether the trees have come for her too. <br /><br />Then Adrien meets green-fingered Hannah and her teenage son Seb. Together, they set out to find Hannah’s forester brother, to reunite Adrien with his wife – and to discover just how deep the forest goes.<br /><br />Their journey will take them to a place of terrible beauty and violence, to the dark heart of nature and the darkness inside themselves.</span>
  <a data-text-id="547698777162327745" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_1608857321" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1608857321" />
    <a title="karen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/45618-karen"><img alt="karen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1596291582p2/45618.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1608857321?book_show_action=true">Apr 12, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="karen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="karen" href="/user/show/45618-karen">karen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/45618-karen?shelf=netgalley">netgalley</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/45618-karen?shelf=nature-will-end-you">nature-will-end-you</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/45618-karen?shelf=hello-foxen">hello-foxen</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1608857321" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3995588143590947863">first of all, we need to take a moment to do some good old-fashioned cover-drooling:<br/><br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/books/1446825691l/27507117.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>happily, this book lives up to how stunning its cover is. <br/><br/>at nearly 500 pages, it's a bit formidable, but once it gets going, it's such an engrossing read, you don't even feel like you're reading a two-inch thick book - you're completely transported into its world, even though it's a world you won't want to visit. <br/><br/>unfortunately, it's <i>our</i> world, with some devastating refurbishments. it's one of the more unusual ap</span>
  <span id="freeText3995588143590947863" style="display:none">first of all, we need to take a moment to do some good old-fashioned cover-drooling:<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/books/1446825691l/27507117.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>happily, this book lives up to how stunning its cover is. <br><br>at nearly 500 pages, it's a bit formidable, but once it gets going, it's such an engrossing read, you don't even feel like you're reading a two-inch thick book - you're completely transported into its world, even though it's a world you won't want to visit. <br><br>unfortunately, it's <i>our</i> world, with some devastating refurbishments. it's one of the more unusual apocalypse scenarios i've read: one night, when people are tucked safely in their homes, sleeping snugly in their beds, the trees come. shooting up from beneath the ground, all at once, fully-formed trees come crashing up, destroying everything in their paths - impaling humans and animals, shattering houses, splintering already-existing trees, turning the world into a primeval forest and then - silence.<br><br>except for the screams.<br><br>the story begins in england, on the night of the trees, where a man named adrien sleeps alone in his house while his wife michelle is away in ireland on a business trip. they had fought before she left, ending in a prolonged sulk from him and exasperation from her and now, with communication impossible, adrien is unable to contact her, to know how far the treepocalypse has spread, whether she is even still alive.<br><br>adrien is no hero. he is fearful, cautious, unambitious, and filled with self-loathing. he knows he has been a disappointing husband to michelle, despite all her indulgent coaxing, giving him the space to find himself and his own path to happiness.<br><br><i>'Little things overwhelm you. Sometimes it's as if everything overwhelms you.'<br><br>'You're right,' he'd said, wiping tears and rainwater alike from his cheeks. 'But in those times the world just seems so damned formidable.'<br><br>With one arm she'd held the umbrella over his head. With the other she'd reached around his waist and held on to him tight. 'I want to help you, Adrien. But you mustn't give up. You can't wait for the world to be perfect before you start living in it.'</i><br><br>and now, on his own, in a decidedly imperfect world, adrien is at a complete loss of how to cope with this most unexpected turn of events. wandering dazed through the chaos of the new world, he sees dead bodies, looted supermarkets, bewildered police, wild creatures skulking through a territory reclaimed by nature, and he is horrified and helpless. he meets hannah and her teenaged son seb, reluctantly agreeing to follow them to hannah's brother zach's place, where he has been living off the grid as a forester and will presumably be unfazed, even delighted, by these arboreal developments. romantically-minded seb encourages adrien to go from there to ireland to find michelle, and adrien agrees; having no intention of following through, but lacking the self-sufficiency to survive on his own. as for hannah, she is enchanted by the appearance of the trees. a vegetarian who adores nature, she doesn't register the impaled bodies, seeing only the beautiful, magical, <i>rightful</i> reinstatement of nature's dominance over civilization. <br><br>adrien's views of nature are more dismal:<br><br><i>How he hated gardening. The falseness of it. Digging out a weed because it wasn't pretty enough. Coming upon the slugs who had melted after digesting pellets. One time blindly reaching into a spray of withered daffodils and finding a bird bleeding to death from someone's pet cat.<br><br>'Mother Nature is a psychopath,' he'd told Michelle, 'and I won't spend my weekends on my hands and knees, painting her toenails.'</i><br><br>over the course of the novel, events transpire which will alter their individual relationships with nature, and ultimately, their most essential characteristics, causing them both to do things that would have been unthinkable before the trees. this is more than just a simple wilderness survival situation. the woods have eerie qualities apart from the nature of their arrival, including spindly twiglike creatures known as whisperers and also kirin, which act as guiding spirits. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1464464267i/19242179._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>in addition, the forest has a tendency to redirect those who wander its paths, and hallucinatory qualities that impart to some a borg-like connectivity to the natural world:<br><br><i>'In the egg it's so sweet. You float in the slime, and when your mother sits in the nest you get so warm.' The man wrapped his arms around himself, a weak smile passing over his face. 'You grow, then. Do all the growing you can in the heat. It makes you want to break out and yell for worms, and you don't know any better and you don't know how cruel things are on the outside.' He shook his head sorrowfully. 'But when you're the mother bird, that's when you know.'</i> <br><br>the story has a distinct fairy-tale cast to it; one of the grimmer ones, where nature is capricious, indifferent, cruel, and occasionally beautiful:<br><br><i>Adrien watched a lone pink petal flitter through the air, then ruin the secrecy of a spiderweb.</i><br><br>but the heart-stealing stars of the book are hiroko, a young japanese girl very well-acclimated to this environment, and her half-tamed fox companion yasuo, most often found curled up in the hood of her sweatshirt as she stalks through the trees, kicking ass and taking names, nursing her own regrets and secrets: <i>Seb had helped her learn to cope with it, but a weight didn't lighten just because you found the strength to carry it.</i><br><br>it's a gorgeous, unpredictable beast of a tale - part epic quest saga, part fairytale, part cautionary tale,with romance, adventure, survival, horror, all of it. and despite being, essentially, a showdown between man and nature, it avoids the reductive preachiness of man = bad, nature = good. <br><br>'cuz nature's got no conception of 'good,' no matter how we anthropomorphize it. nature'll rip your heart out and devour its weakest and it'll outlast us all. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1464464267i/19242180._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1528725630i/25720315.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><a href="http://bloggycomelately.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">come to my blog!</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="3995588143590947863" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1712338714" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1712338714" />
    <a title="carol. " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3978225-carol"><img alt="carol. " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307934801p2/3978225.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1712338714?book_show_action=true">Jul 28, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="carol. " class="user" itemprop="url" name="carol. " href="/user/show/3978225-carol">carol. </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3978225-carol?shelf=end-of-the-world-as-we-know-it">end-of-the-world-as-we-know-it</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3978225-carol?shelf=should-buy">should-buy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3978225-carol?shelf=to-re-read">to-re-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1712338714" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9379960400144936644"><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16902.Walden' title='Walden by Henry David Thoreau' rel='nofollow noopener'>Walden</a> meets the apocalypse.<br/><br/>Don't read this book. You will complain that there is no plot, and it's true, there isn't much, not really. It's entirely allegorical, and you hate allegorical books. You will be irritated by the characters: one full of daffy, unrealistic optimism, one self-pitying and full of so much inertia he can barely move. You will hate that it isn't grounded in real possibility, because what is possibly real about millions of trees sprouting full grown from the soil overnight? </span>
  <span id="freeText9379960400144936644" style="display:none"><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16902.Walden" title="Walden by Henry David Thoreau" rel="nofollow noopener">Walden</a> meets the apocalypse.<br /><br />Don't read this book. You will complain that there is no plot, and it's true, there isn't much, not really. It's entirely allegorical, and you hate allegorical books. You will be irritated by the characters: one full of daffy, unrealistic optimism, one self-pitying and full of so much inertia he can barely move. You will hate that it isn't grounded in real possibility, because what is possibly real about millions of trees sprouting full grown from the soil overnight? The dialogue will likely annoy you, consisting as it does of travelers making traveling decisions, holding each other accountable, offering solace. You will find the forays into magical realism, into myth and fairy tale distracting, and wish that they would just hurry up and reach their destination already.<br /><br />For me, however, it was the perfect book at the perfect time, a dovetailing of my own green tendencies and a love for the end of the world as we know it. An apocalyptic fairy tale that has quiet and solid emotional truth about choices, self-determination and risk. Early on were parts where two of the main characters, Hannah and Adrien, significantly annoyed, but it is quickly apparent that such behaviors were both part of their role in the story and an evolving point. There are two teenagers, Seb and Hiroko that help provide balance to the heaviness of the adults. And that was one of the most interesting things about this book, that I believed these characters. I believed their take on the issues they confronted. Rather than being bored, I was fascinated by both the smallness of some issues--a mother's reaction to a child's lifestyle choice--and the largeness of other ones. I believed them all.<br /><b><br />"Both the town and the woods were quiet that morning. Where, in the days before, they had been filled with cries and sobbing and the sounds of things crashing into the dirt, now there was just the simmer of the leaves, and grey faces watching the three new travelers without expression as they passed. Adrien's final sight of the place where he had lived for so many years was an electricity hub fished off the ground, dangling its cables like a jellyfish caught in a net. Then the town was gone, and he was walking after Hannah down a ruined route of tarmac, and trees were leaning over him from every direction."<br /></b><br /><br />I think people with a high tolerance for fairy tale-like worlds and plotting will enjoy this. Interestingly, I found its journey of the self reminding me of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/126192.Mythago_Wood__Mythago_Wood___1_" title="Mythago Wood (Mythago Wood, #1) by Robert Holdstock" rel="nofollow noopener">Mythago Wood</a>, a book I didn't care for at all. It also reminds me more than a bit of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9361589.The_Night_Circus" title="The Night Circus by Erin Morgenstern" rel="nofollow noopener">The Night Circus</a>, less in the way of beautiful imagery but with many similarities in a meandering sort of plot. Don't read this book if you don't have a high tolerance for The Long Walk(s).<br /><br />Four and a half stars, rounding up because it's been rare these days that a story has so absorbed me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9379960400144936644" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1370593783" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1370593783" />
    <a title="Kai" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/41788524-kai"><img alt="Kai" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1573562141p2/41788524.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1370593783?book_show_action=true">Aug 22, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kai" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kai" href="/user/show/41788524-kai">Kai</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41788524-kai?shelf=owned">owned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41788524-kai?shelf=2016">2016</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1370593783" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8229617065158742268"><i>"The world keeps no secrets. Look it in the eye if you can. Everything is there to see."</i><br/><br/>It happens in the deepest hours of the night: the Trees claim back what once was theirs. Suddenly the world is a forest and civilisation is shattered. Adrien has to leave what he once called home to find his wife on the other side of the sea. But it's what he will find at the heart of the forest that will change him forever.<br/>On his journey through the Trees Adrien meets Hannah and her son Seb. Heading in the s</span>
  <span id="freeText8229617065158742268" style="display:none"><i>"The world keeps no secrets. Look it in the eye if you can. Everything is there to see."</i><br /><br />It happens in the deepest hours of the night: the Trees claim back what once was theirs. Suddenly the world is a forest and civilisation is shattered. Adrien has to leave what he once called home to find his wife on the other side of the sea. But it's what he will find at the heart of the forest that will change him forever.<br />On his journey through the Trees Adrien meets Hannah and her son Seb. Heading in the same direction they're looking for Hannah's brother, a forester who surely knows what to do in a world, where everything is turned upside down. The trio later stumbles upon Hiroko, a Japanese schoolgirl with impressive survival skills, that soon captures Seb's fascination.<br /><br />I've waited a long time for this novel, and it was completely worthwhile. Ever since <i>The Girl with the Glass Feet</i> Ali Shaw won over my heart and now he returned to claim it once again.<br /><br />This book is lots of things. A wondrous journey of self-discovery, a haunting fairytale, a story of friendship and love and a telling of murder and justice.<br />At first I was fascinated by the apocalyptic event that introduces and leads through this novel. But the deeper the story goes the more there is to see. There's so much going on!<br />The novel takes some surprisingly dark turns which build up suspense, fear and most of all curiosity.<br />The author intertwines different genres in the most amazing way.<br /><br />I could gush about it forever but I don't want to say too much.<br />Ali Shaw is a master of magical realism and therefore deserves much more attention.<br /><br />And if all of this didn't convince you to read this book than you should have a long look at the beautiful and stunning cover.<br /><br /><a href="https://www.instagram.com/fantasticbooksandwheretofindem/" rel="nofollow noopener">Find more of my books on Instagram</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="8229617065158742268" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1636934377" />
    <a title="Jess ❈Harbinger of Blood-Soaked Rainbows❈" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7125124-jess-harbinger-of-blood-soaked-rainbows"><img alt="Jess ❈Harbinger of Blood-Soaked Rainbows❈" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1440512431p2/7125124.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1636934377?book_show_action=true">May 13, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jess ❈Harbinger of Blood-Soaked Rainbows❈" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jess ❈Harbinger of Blood-Soaked Rainbows❈" href="/user/show/7125124-jess-harbinger-of-blood-soaked-rainbows">Jess ❈Harbinger of Blood-Soaked Rainbows❈</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/29483633-the-trees">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7125124-jess-harbinger-of-blood-soaked-rainbows?shelf=netgalley">netgalley</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7125124-jess-harbinger-of-blood-soaked-rainbows?shelf=a-bit-o-magical-realism">a-bit-o-magical-realism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7125124-jess-harbinger-of-blood-soaked-rainbows?shelf=for-kindle">for-kindle</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7125124-jess-harbinger-of-blood-soaked-rainbows?shelf=4-stars">4-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7125124-jess-harbinger-of-blood-soaked-rainbows?shelf=sci-fi-post-apoc">sci-fi-post-apoc</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7125124-jess-harbinger-of-blood-soaked-rainbows?shelf=that-cover-though">that-cover-though</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7125124-jess-harbinger-of-blood-soaked-rainbows?shelf=bizarrelandia">bizarrelandia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7125124-jess-harbinger-of-blood-soaked-rainbows?shelf=popsugar-2016">popsugar-2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7125124-jess-harbinger-of-blood-soaked-rainbows?shelf=to-reread">to-reread</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1636934377" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2194958985230129863"><b>FINAL REVIEW TIME</b><br/>Sorry for all the bumping guys!<br/><br/>That cover is the most stunning cover I've seen in ages! And 90% of the reason I requested this title. The other 10% is for the author, Ali Shaw, from whom I've not read anything else, but whose book, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6063110.The_Girl_With_Glass_Feet' title='The Girl With Glass Feet by Ali Shaw' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Girl With Glass Feet</a> has been recommended to me for years because of another book I'd read and loved, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/248461221?book_show_action=false' rel='nofollow noopener'>Of Bees and Mist</a>, which also has a drool-worthy cover and is a darker side to a fairy tale.<br/><br/>I love magical realism. I love survivalist stories. I </span>
  <span id="freeText2194958985230129863" style="display:none"><b>FINAL REVIEW TIME</b><br>Sorry for all the bumping guys!<br><br>That cover is the most stunning cover I've seen in ages! And 90% of the reason I requested this title. The other 10% is for the author, Ali Shaw, from whom I've not read anything else, but whose book, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6063110.The_Girl_With_Glass_Feet" title="The Girl With Glass Feet by Ali Shaw" rel="nofollow noopener">The Girl With Glass Feet</a> has been recommended to me for years because of another book I'd read and loved, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/248461221?book_show_action=false" rel="nofollow noopener">Of Bees and Mist</a>, which also has a drool-worthy cover and is a darker side to a fairy tale.<br><br>I love magical realism. I love survivalist stories. I love dark fairy tales and post-apocalyptic worlds. I love dynamic characters and mythological creatures and internal struggles.<br><br>I really liked this book which has a bit of everything I just listed above. It is a book that is going to stick to my ribs long after I've finished.<br><br>But I'm still not really sure how to write a proper review. And I am going to have limited time over the next few days so I'm going to write this review a piece at a time. So bear with me. The reason this book lost a whole star is because though it contains everything that I love that is listed above, the book has a little bit of an identity crisis. If I had a complaint it would be that this book mashes so many different genres together, it can all be a little jarring at times. And that's not to say it took away from my enjoyment of it, but rather it disturbed the flow of the story. There were almost too many directions, too many themes, too many conflicts all competing for space within 500 pages and a handful of characters. This book literally oscillates between an epic post-apocalyptic adventure tale, a gritty coming of age saga, and of all things, a fairytale. There are some really really dark and shadowy elements and themes here, some violence and disturbing imagery, and a lot of that whispered about, inky black pieces of human nature, but there is also a bit of whimsy and beauty and a lightness about it despite all the dark. And though I don't necessarily think that these competing genres always meshed well, something that can be said is that it wasn't predictable. I honestly had no idea what was going to happen ever. It was a story that was completely outside the box, and I appreciate an author who is able to kind of throw all the rules of their genre to the wind.<br><br>All of you who know me know I lose my shit over <i>The Walking Dead</i>. It is the best TV show I have ever seen since <i>Twin Peaks</i>. It is not really a show about zombies, but rather survival. How surviving in a world no longer your own can change you, change your very core, make you do things and say things that you never could have seen yourself do or say, not in a million years. Alliances are formed and broken, relationships are forged, strained, tightened, let go. It is a show that strives to answer the question "What do you do in order to survive for just one more day when everything in the world is fighting against your survival?"<br><br>If <i>The Walking Dead</i> is a big badass dude from 'Murica, then <i>The Trees</i> is its smaller, tree-hugging, hipster English cousin.<br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1464874683ra/19287010.gif" width="375" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Because even though they look like completely different beasts altogether, they share many of the same themes, struggles, twists, turns, and realities. They look different, act different, speak different, but when it comes down to it there is a strong familial resemblance that is difficult to ignore and one of the reasons why I ate this book up.<br>We have some of this...ish<br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1464978428ra/19300298.gif" width="340" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>And some of this...ish <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1464978428i/19300299._SX540_.jpg" width="378" height="278" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>A little of this...ish<br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1464978428ra/19300300.gif" width="390" height="200" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>And a little of that....ish<br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1464978428ra/19300301.gif" width="160" height="270" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>And a lot of this<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1464978428i/19300302._SX540_.jpg" width="390" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>It was a wicked good ride.<br><br>It is also important to note that just as <i>The Walking Dead</i> really isn't about zombies, <i>The Trees</i> isn't really about trees.<br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1464874683ra/19287011.gif" width="385" height="212" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>And I'm not saying that trees and zombies are the same thing, but they both serve, in their respective stories, as not necessarily characters, but as a backdrop to a world that is no longer the same. Zombies take over the world, killing a lot of humanity, and therefore the survivors are forced to shift gears and figure out how to survive in this new world. Same happens in this novel except that instead of the undead, massive trees erupt all over the country, uprooting buildings and other trees, killing a lot of humanity, and in essence creating one big massive forest instead of the progressive urban environment everyone has become used to. And again in this novel, the survivors are forced to stop, switch gears, and figure out how to survive in this new world which includes a lot of reverting back to some primitive ways of living, hunting, gathering, boiling water, roughing it to the extreme.<br><br>I kept thinking about how this book would make a really interesting film. Not a huge blockbuster, but a quiet little English independent film that comes out in limited release and doesn't really get the attention it deserves, but appeals to its audience: people who appreciate film/literature for not just story alone, but for beauty of cinematography, interesting thematic elements, the beauty of the premise, the complicated and nuanced characters, and not necessarily a fast paced storyline a'la <i>The Avengers</i> or <i>Jurassic World</i>. <br><br>I do need to touch on these characters because they are a big part of what make this story magical. Stories about the changing of the human core into something else wouldn't be very effective without human cores to adapt. The MC is a man named Adrien Thomas, who in my movie version would be played by Ricky Gervais.<br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1464978428ra/19300303.gif" width="350" height="200" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Adrien is sad, fearful, middle-aged, boring, full of self-loathing and cynicism, and is most definitely not a hero. Definitely not a man to take the time to grow his own garden, let alone walk across England, cross the ocean, and trek all over Ireland in order to find his estranged wife Michelle. Though with help from his new friend Hannah (played by Kate Winslet)<br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1464978428ra/19300304.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg">a bit of a green-thumbed, tree-hugging, well-intentioned nature lover, Adrien decides to do just that. Though Adrien decides to travel to find Michelle (played by Anne Hathaway)<br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1464978428ra/19300305.gif" width="389" height="190" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>he does not fully commit to it, nor does he know what he's going to do once he finds her. It starts to become evident that Michelle is bright and beautiful and successful and loves Adrien fiercely but its a bit of a puzzle to try and figure out exactly what she sees in him. Even Adrien seems to question her love and loyalty and it has caused many fights between the two, the last one never really being resolved and landing on the morning Michelle departs for Ireland on a business trip, a few days before the trees come. She tries to fix things between the two of them before leaving, but Adrien just huffs and puffs and mopes around and won't look her in the eye and won't talk to her. (In fact, Adrien is shockingly not the most likable character in the world). He keeps imagining an affair happening between Michelle and her attractive and confident Irish boss, Roland (played by Liam Neeson)<br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1464978428ra/19300306.gif" width="300" height="212" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>and believes so fiercely that this could happen because Michelle is just too good for him. Along for the ride is Hannah's teenaged son Seb, as unlike his nature-loving mother as one could be. Seb feels most at home in the world of the internet and technology and website building, not the world of the great outdoors. I cast Asa Butterfield as Seb.<br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1464978428ra/19300307.gif" width="390" height="231" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>I thought Seb would be a cliche of a boring sullen teenager forced to endure his mother's nature ramblings, but Seb really begins to grow once they encounter a teenaged Japanese girl named Hiroko (I honestly picture Devon Aoki, but I'm thinking she's too old)<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1464978428i/19300308.jpg" width="375" height="250" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>and her adopted fox kit Yasuo<br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1464978428ra/19300309.gif" width="376" height="221" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>who quickly became my absolute favorite characters of the bunch. They are all great and each one shows tremendous growth throughout the novel. I loved reading as each one was pulled and strained by circumstances, loss, new love, old love, familial responsibilities. Each has a conflict they need to overcome, and they all act completely and utterly human. That is probably by biggest praise of this book is the absolute <i>humanness</i> of it. There is nothing inherently good or bad about any of them and they all act the way they feel they should. That is a hard thing to pull off in a book with this subject matter. Even the antagonists have such a human quality to them. There is an empathy to be had with each character and reading this I began to know exactly who they were and what they became and why they behaved the way they did and made the decisions they had. I didn't always like or agree with everything that happened, but I respected the author for making it happen, nonetheless. These characters were the true gems of this book and made this reading experience one I will always remember.<br><br>Lastly, I must touch on the whimsical aspect of this novel because it makes up a large part of it, but doesn't always jive with the other bits. Adrien is (or was, before the trees came) a high school English teacher, more interested in his books than in people, friends, relationships, and the students he teaches. His favorite is Shakespeare's <i>A Midsummer Night's Dream</i>, a play that analyzes chaos vs. structure, magic vs. realism, whimsy vs. steadfastness. In this beloved play, the woods and the city represent chaos and structure, respectively. It kind of goes without saying as to why. This famous play makes several appearances in this novel, and think it deserves a bit of a shout-out because comparing that play to this novel helped me with connections I don't think I would have gotten otherwise. As that play is one of my favorite works of literature of all time, I enjoyed this little nod to the classic. If you are not as familiar with that work, then sorry I cannot help you with the little Easter eggs I enjoyed reading.<br><br>I spent the better part of this review discussing the post-apocalyptic aspect of huge trees springing up everywhere and making the world one big giant forest. A forest where chaos definitely happens. However, giant trees are not the only thing that spring out of nowhere in this new world. Like Shakespeare's classic, there are creatures that live in the woods. And not just squirrels, wolves, and chipmunks either.<br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1465439399ra/19355780.gif" width="300" height="210" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>But as it always seems with magical stories, for every good and light magical being, there seems to be one of equal darkness. And this story is no exception. Trying to figure out the light from the dark-- well, that's a horse of a different color. Oh yeah, speaking of horses of a different color, guess what else roams these enchanted woods?<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1465439399i/19355781._SX540_.png" width="375" height="250" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Mythical kirin show up every so often, and seem to be sort of physical and spiritual guides for our characters, only showing up when they truly need their guidance. I loved these creatures and though their presences are short-lived, really felt them to be well-rendered, imaginative, and truly beautiful. It makes me want to research their lore on my own.<br><br>Other than the kirin, other strange creatures that roam the woods are not so clearly defined, and I found their presence interesting but slightly odd. Especially their connection with our main characters which becomes more and more clear as the story progresses toward its conclusion, was confusing and intrusive and this is the biggest issue I had with the entire book. I didn't hate it, but felt like for a book which was so dependent on the rawness of human emotion and survival, it depended a little too much on magic in order to tie up loose ends. It's really a minor squabble, but as it is what comprised a huge part of the ending, I still find a need to discuss its oddness.<br><br>Other than the magicky bits, I really enjoyed the ending of this novel and found that parts of it really struck chords with me and I thought about them long after I finished. That is what the power of good writing and great characters does. It's something I still find it hard to express in words, it is just something that is felt. And I continue to feel a lot of things about this novel, and that's what makes it a success.<br><br>And that's all folks. Thanks for bearing with me though this monstrous review that I'm sure is too long and boring for all of you to read, but the whole point of my ramblings is that you should read this book. It is definitely worth it, not only for that gorgeous cover, but for all the gorgeous words too.<br><br><i>I received an ARC of this novel from Netgalley in exchange for an honest review.</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="2194958985230129863" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1997273840?book_show_action=true">Nov 20, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Charlotte May" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Charlotte May" href="/user/show/14002983-charlotte-may">Charlotte May</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781408862308" href="/book/show/31794085-the-trees">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14002983-charlotte-may?shelf=dystopia">dystopia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14002983-charlotte-may?shelf=supernatural-paranormal">supernatural-paranormal</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14002983-charlotte-may?shelf=end-of-the-world">end-of-the-world</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1997273840" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15063765745294251364"><i> “His name was Adrien Thomas. He fell asleep with a whimper and a snore. And somewhere out there in the darkness, something creaked.” </i> <br/><br/>Ok, I didn’t like this. But I didn’t dislike it enough to give up, so I’m going with 2 stars ⭐️ <br/><br/>I adore The Girl With Glass Feet by Ali Shaw. It is one of my favourite books. I was keen to try something else by him. <br/>It’s a great concept, overnight the entire world is covered by trees - breaking up through the ground destroying houses, cars and lives. <br/><br/>Adrien Thoma</span>
  <span id="freeText15063765745294251364" style="display:none"><i> “His name was Adrien Thomas. He fell asleep with a whimper and a snore. And somewhere out there in the darkness, something creaked.” </i> <br /><br />Ok, I didn’t like this. But I didn’t dislike it enough to give up, so I’m going with 2 stars ⭐️ <br /><br />I adore The Girl With Glass Feet by Ali Shaw. It is one of my favourite books. I was keen to try something else by him. <br />It’s a great concept, overnight the entire world is covered by trees - breaking up through the ground destroying houses, cars and lives. <br /><br />Adrien Thomas is not a brave man, by his own admission. When the trees arrive he is terrified. When he meets Hannah and her son Seb they couldn’t be more different. Hannah sees the trees as a blessing, a chance for the world to get back to nature. <br /><br />They travel together, their end goal to reach Ireland, where Adrien’s wife was. Along the way they meet Hiroko - a Japanese girl away on a school trip but very handy with a slingshot. <br /><br />So my main gripes were that it was so gruesome, loads of animal killings and human killings. Which normally I do but find if it’s fantasy - but this all felt a bit too real. Not a lot happened plot wise really and the ending was just fucking weird. <br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ So Adrien becomes one with the big Earth tree? Or whatever the hell it was. So damn peculiar.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <br /><br />So overall, good concept, sadly not a good execution for me. </span>

<div id="review_1637621427" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1637621427" />
    <a title="Puck" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18562856-puck"><img alt="Puck" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1498403772p2/18562856.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1637621427?book_show_action=true">May 14, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Puck" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Puck" href="/user/show/18562856-puck">Puck</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18562856-puck?shelf=diverse-reads">diverse-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18562856-puck?shelf=underrated">underrated</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18562856-puck?shelf=cover-love">cover-love</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18562856-puck?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1637621427" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16449456029356967959"><b> 4 stars for this wonderful, fairy-like survival tale. </b><br/><br/>Wow, this book was something else. Not only does it have one of the most beautiful covers I have ever seen, but this book also tells an unusual but enchanting story. It’s a mix of an apocalyptic survival story, a magical fairy tale, and a heroic quest in one, told in less than 500 pages. Yes: it’s quite a beast of a tale, and the pace of this story isn’t fast and action-packed, but <b>The Trees</b> is such an engrossing read that you won’t notice h</span>
  <span id="freeText16449456029356967959" style="display:none"><b> 4 stars for this wonderful, fairy-like survival tale. </b><br /><br />Wow, this book was something else. Not only does it have one of the most beautiful covers I have ever seen, but this book also tells an unusual but enchanting story. It’s a mix of an apocalyptic survival story, a magical fairy tale, and a heroic quest in one, told in less than 500 pages. Yes: it’s quite a beast of a tale, and the pace of this story isn’t fast and action-packed, but <b>The Trees</b> is such an engrossing read that you won’t notice how thick this book is. You only want to read more about the world and about our four leading characters, who all are strong and impressive in their own way.<br /><br />First, the setting of this book is in our own world. One night, without warning, a gigantic forest bursts full-grown out of the earth. Large tree trunks break through buildings and roads and cause death, destruction, and chaos. If you don’t possess any nature knowledge or survival skills, you’re doomed. Our main character, Adrien Thomas, is one of those doomed people, but luckily for him he runs into nature-loving Hannah and her son Seb. <br />When they invite Adrien to come with them on their expedition into the forest to find Hannah’s brother, he joins them in the hesitant hope that he can find his wife. On their trip the three run into Hiroko, a young Japanese girl who knows her way around the wild, and whose practical mind-set is a more powerful weapon than Hannah’s plant knowledge. <br />Because this forest isn’t as inviting and beautiful as the forests we are familiar with: it has shadowy and dark parts where nature (and people!) take what they need without giving anything in return. Not because nature is evil and unjust, but because that’s how it works. The trees want to live too, not just the humans. <br /><br /><i>
  <blockquote> Eoin raised an eyebrow. 'You're right, you know. The sea can be a mean old bitch, but she makes no bones about it. A shark is a shark, my old captain used to say.' <br /><br />Hannah nodded. ‘A shark is a shark, I like that. It’s not the same in the woods. Even something like a wolf, it…it disguises what it really is. Makes you think it’s beautiful. Even the trees starve each other of the light. I’ve always called myself a person who loves nature, but I suppose what I’ve thought of as nature has always been trees, mammals, flowers…so much life you can distract yourself from the death.’ </blockquote>
</i> <br /><br />Oh Hannah. Of all four leading characters, I felt and loved her the most. At the beginning of the story Hannah is a gentle, positive woman who loves nature, who even welcomes the new green landscape and sees it as a way of nature restoring balance. During the story however things happen to her that not only change her look on nature, but also on justice and fairness. She has to swallow some bitter pills but her character strength is amazing. <br /><br />Hiroko and Adrien go through some powerful change as well. Hiroko’s first impression is of a standoffish girl who kicks ass and hides her emotions from everyone. However, thanks to her relationship with Seb, and her pet-fox Yasuo, secrets from her past slowly come to the surface that she has to confront. <br />Adrien is an unusual main character and our ‘hero’, although you wouldn’t call him that. He is more of a follower than a leader. He’s cautious and filled with self-loathing, and I’d even say he’s dealing with a depression. Despite his wish to find his wife Michelle, Adrien thinks more of how he failed her as a husband and how she’s better off without him, than of how he’s going to deal with the treepocalypse. <br />That makes him not the most exciting person to read about, but as someone who struggles with self-doubt as well, Adrien is the character I most identified with. <br /><br /><i> <blockquote> [Michelle:] ‘Little things overwhelm you. Sometimes it's as if everything overwhelms you.”<br /><br />'You're right,' he'd said, wiping tears and rainwater alike from his cheeks. 'But in those times the world just seems so damned formidable.'<br /><br />With one arm she'd held the umbrella over his head. With the other she'd reached around his waist and held on to him tight. 'I want to help you, Adrien. But you mustn't give up. You can't wait for the world to be perfect before you start living in it.' </blockquote></i> <br /><br />I call this story a fairy tale, although the magic elements stay limited to a couple of mythological creatures that burst ‘out of the earth’ at the same time as the forest. The unicorn-like Kirin and the ‘Whisperers’ (I kept thinking of the little Kodama from <i>Princess Mononoke</i>) appear from time to time, but apart from the finale, they do very little. <br />It’s kind of disappointing that <b>Ali Shaw</b> hasn’t done more with the forest folklore, because last year a book like “Uprooted” showed how terrifying a magical forest can be. <br /><br />But on a broader level it’s the unbalanced mix of all the story elements, not just the magical one, that keeps me from giving these book 5 stars. Shaw brings together so many different story ingredients in this book – like dystopian, mythological, and epic-quest ones - that none of them work out perfectly. The worldwide forest that suddenly appears comes as a big shock, but afterwards never forms a real threat. The Whisperes are there from the start, but only at the end of the novel you get a glimpse of their purpose. And while Adrien grows out to be the hero, Hannah and Hiroko get the most character development. <br /><br />But on the bright side: the tempo of this story is a slow one, so none of the story-elements crash into each other or clash. We can calmly follow Adrien, Hannah, Seb, and Hiroko into the wild forest and see them all come face-to-face with overgrown reality, (magical) cruelty, and their own inner demons. The world has been drastically changed after all, and so all four change too, slow but steady. <br />The ending of this book brought a tear to my eye and a bittersweet smile on my face, so if that isn’t a recommendation, I don’t know what is.<br /><br />This is Ali Shaw's third book - the two earlier ones are both shorter than this one - but with <b>The Trees</b> the writer clearly shows what his creativity is capable of. It's quite a trip, but this epic story is really worth its pages.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16449456029356967959" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3234906817" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3234906817" />
    <a title="Kinga" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/110812-kinga"><img alt="Kinga" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1491308194p2/110812.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3234906817?book_show_action=true">Apr 10, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kinga" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kinga" href="/user/show/110812-kinga">Kinga</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Kinga by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/45618-karen">karen</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/110812-kinga?shelf=pub-2016">pub-2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/110812-kinga?shelf=kinga-and-victor-buddy-reads">kinga-and-victor-buddy-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3234906817" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16408162161850759373">I asked karen for a recommendation for a book to read during this catastrophe we are riding through currently. I didn’t want any realistic family drama – how can I take their petty problems seriously given our current circumstances? I wanted an apocalypse, but of a different kind, so that it doesn’t hit too close to home. I said: give me nature, survival, forests, maybe some sea but mostly trees, please. <br/><br/>Rightly, she recommended The Trees.<br/><br/>The apocalypse our characters are finding themselves in i</span>
  <span id="freeText16408162161850759373" style="display:none">I asked karen for a recommendation for a book to read during this catastrophe we are riding through currently. I didn’t want any realistic family drama – how can I take their petty problems seriously given our current circumstances? I wanted an apocalypse, but of a different kind, so that it doesn’t hit too close to home. I said: give me nature, survival, forests, maybe some sea but mostly trees, please. <br /><br />Rightly, she recommended The Trees.<br /><br />The apocalypse our characters are finding themselves in is the opposite of our current one. While we have been forced inside, they have been forced outside – very literally. One morning they wake to realise everything has been covered by trees which sprung out overnight, destroying their homes and skewering many of the inhabitants. This has probably happened all over the world, the characters suspect but can’t verify as you can’t see very far in a dense forest.<br /><br />Yes, it’s very much magical realism, and like any magical realism you just gotta get on board with the premise and suspend your disbelief. There will be no rational explanations forthcoming. <br />The characters we are saddled with for the duration of this journey represent the entire spectrum of the attitude towards nature – from the romanticising flower-child kind, through a ruthless and apt survivalist, through an adaptable teenager, to our main (anti)hero - very attached to his creature comforts and resentful of the apocalypse for daring to try to shake him out of his inertia. <br /><br />This book is trying to be many things at once. Sometimes it is a sinister fairy tale, that swiftly turns itself into a gory thriller in the vein of The Walking Dead, to then again return to a more meditative piece on what nature’s nature is, and what relationship we could possibly have with it. This concoction of genres usually works and only occasionally jars. <br /><br />What I particularly liked about this novel was its unlikely hero (the previously mentioned ‘anti-hero’) who routinely fails to live up to expectations, even as these are constantly lowered. And this isn’t a zero to hero kind of story, where the character finds inner strength and bravely saves the world. What really saves it is precisely his lack of ego, his indecisiveness and willingness to just drift with the flow. Because maybe the world has had enough of men trying to bend it to their will.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16408162161850759373" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1738999201" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1738999201" />
    <a title="Arielle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6762949-arielle"><img alt="Arielle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1452561487p2/6762949.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1738999201?book_show_action=true">Aug 25, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Arielle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Arielle" href="/user/show/6762949-arielle">Arielle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6762949-arielle?shelf=fantasy-unusual">fantasy-unusual</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6762949-arielle?shelf=forests-ngahere-trees">forests-ngahere-trees</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6762949-arielle?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6762949-arielle?shelf=dystopia-and-post-apocalypse">dystopia-and-post-apocalypse</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1738999201" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16397102742529868887">Eerie, lush and hauntingly lovely - doesn't sound exactly like a post-apocalyptic novel, right? Certainly not the "lovely" part. In fact, it's felt a little pre-apocalyptic recently, what with a Trump-filled week being swiftly followed by yet another devastating earthquake in the South. <br/><br/>Some people have even suggested, half-jokingly, that the quakes came in response to the political and humanitarian turmoil; I can't entirely agree. If Nature really were to respond to our pettiness, I would expec</span>
  <span id="freeText16397102742529868887" style="display:none">Eerie, lush and hauntingly lovely - doesn't sound exactly like a post-apocalyptic novel, right? Certainly not the "lovely" part. In fact, it's felt a little pre-apocalyptic recently, what with a Trump-filled week being swiftly followed by yet another devastating earthquake in the South. <br /><br />Some people have even suggested, half-jokingly, that the quakes came in response to the political and humanitarian turmoil; I can't entirely agree. If Nature really were to respond to our pettiness, I would expect it to be more like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/27507117.The_Trees" title="The Trees by Ali Shaw" rel="nofollow noopener">The Trees</a>: total, all-encompassing, complete. <br /><br />The destruction here is simply of the current world, urban spaces replaced entirely by new growth. While most (or many) humans seem to have survived, how long they will do so is entirely down to adaptation. I've seen reviews that speak of a malevolence in the coming of the trees; I found the opposite. The real terror, true horror comes from the humans themselves.<br /><br />Adrian is a remarkably unlikeable character, and if not for a somewhat surprising twist, he would have brought down the book entirely. Luckily book itself more than makes up for him - it's a very, very, very good read.<br /><br />Note: I have to point you towards Carol.'s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1712338714" rel="nofollow noopener">most excellent review</a>, where she coins the phrase "apocalyptic fairytale" - never such an apt description have I found on Goodreads!</span>
  <a data-text-id="16397102742529868887" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1887223079" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1887223079" />
    <a title="Jess ❈Harbinger of Blood-Soaked Rainbows❈" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7125124-jess-harbinger-of-blood-soaked-rainbows"><img alt="Jess ❈Harbinger of Blood-Soaked Rainbows❈" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1440512431p2/7125124.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1887223079?book_show_action=true">Jan 21, 2017</a>

<div id="review_3623059349" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3623059349" />
    <a title="Jennifer" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1188456-jennifer"><img alt="Jennifer" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1482893253p2/1188456.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3623059349?book_show_action=true">Nov 06, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jennifer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jennifer" href="/user/show/1188456-jennifer">Jennifer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3623059349" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13180599881417430203">This is kind of a mashup between the environmental themes and surreal visuals of Miyazaki films and <i>The Lord of the Flies</i>. (Although, honestly, what post-apocalyptic books are <b>not</b> about the darkness in man's heart?) As in <i>My Neighbor Totoro</i>, the trees grow up out of nowhere overnight, like magic. But in <b>The Trees</b>, they grow up through houses and impale people like shishkebabs. I can't say I enjoyed reading it or liked any of the characters, but it got under my skin. <br/><br/>Apocalypse via overnight fore</span>
  <span id="freeText13180599881417430203" style="display:none">This is kind of a mashup between the environmental themes and surreal visuals of Miyazaki films and <i>The Lord of the Flies</i>. (Although, honestly, what post-apocalyptic books are <b>not</b> about the darkness in man's heart?) As in <i>My Neighbor Totoro</i>, the trees grow up out of nowhere overnight, like magic. But in <b>The Trees</b>, they grow up through houses and impale people like shishkebabs. I can't say I enjoyed reading it or liked any of the characters, but it got under my skin. <br /><br />Apocalypse via overnight forest growth doesn't seem like the likeliest way human civilization will end, but it's an interesting one. Even ordinary trees can push up pavements, crack sewer pipelines, and cleave buildings in two. This ending also strikes me as fully justified, given how many millions of acres of forest humans have destroyed, and kind of awesome in the old sense of the word: <br /><br /><blockquote>And then, from somewhere underground, a yawning creak began. Those who heard it dismissed it for the soughing of a branch in the wind, or of a telephone mast in need of repair. Yet both its sound and its source were deeper than that. It was a moan of some lumbering presence stirring, as of something drawing towards consciousness from overlong hibernation.</blockquote><br /><br />As a misanthopic botanist, I figured I would be rooting for Team Tree all the way. But <b>The Trees</b> is never that obvious, and within an overarching man vs. nature conflict that definitely doesn't go as expected, there is plenty of conflict between and within the characters as they try to adapt to their new lives. Our anti-hero is Adrien, who is (generously speaking) a waste of oxygen at the outset - a self-pitying drunkard, coward, saboteur of himself and what he loves best. He ends up traveling with his polar opposite, hippie vegetarian earth-loving Hannah (who is convinced that the trees are here to save humans from themselves), her son, and a wayward Japanese student, separated from her class on an unforgettable trip abroad.<br /><br />Theirs is a meandering journey across a transformed England to find Adrien's wife in Ireland. There are encounters with humans who are dealing well with the new normal, and others who are decidedly not; there are glimpses of mythical animals and strange visions of branch-like creatures. There's something archetypal about many of these fleeting encounters, a hint of fairy tale or Greek myth in the helpful vicar, not-so-friendly hunter, lunatic prophet. <br /><br />The writing that brings this brave new world to life is evocative, as often as not capped with unexpected gore or decay:<br /><blockquote>Water had flushed rot and dust up from under the dereliction but, finding its onward passage dammed by swathes of wreckage, had swirled back on itself and half submerged the town. The trees that stood in the streets looked engorged and drunk, their leaves dripping now and then into the water. Blanched branches floated on an opaque surface, and when Hiroko shot a duck she did not keep it, for its feathers were bubbled with noxious suds.</blockquote> <br /><br />Incidentally, this is the second book I've read recently about the coming of age of a pudgy middle-aged man (the other being <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3379157494" rel="nofollow noopener">The House in the Cerulean Sea</a>), and I much prefer the squick and unease of <b>The Trees</b>, which is almost exactly the opposite of a feel-good book. This is one weird, insidiously discombobulating read in which beauty and violence are finely balanced, and there are no easy characterizations or solutions. I didn't like it, but I didn't need to in order to find it interesting.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13180599881417430203" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1683736668" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1683736668" />
    <a title="Sloane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/38246135-sloane"><img alt="Sloane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593622623p2/38246135.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1683736668?book_show_action=true">Jun 30, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sloane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sloane" href="/user/show/38246135-sloane">Sloane</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1683736668" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17130329509351711384">Bottom line:<br/>A lifeless read. Apocalyptic fiction usually opens the door to literary ingenuity and critical observation on human nature and modernity, but “The Trees” has the same level of urgency, intrigue, and intellectual breadth as a primary school nature hike. <br/><br/>Shaw asks the right questions. In a society glued to screens and indoor environments, how prepared is man to face environmental change and catastrophe? Is murder only natural—a part of the life cycle? <br/><br/>So what deflates the book? The she</span>
  <span id="freeText17130329509351711384" style="display:none">Bottom line:<br />A lifeless read. Apocalyptic fiction usually opens the door to literary ingenuity and critical observation on human nature and modernity, but “The Trees” has the same level of urgency, intrigue, and intellectual breadth as a primary school nature hike. <br /><br />Shaw asks the right questions. In a society glued to screens and indoor environments, how prepared is man to face environmental change and catastrophe? Is murder only natural—a part of the life cycle? <br /><br />So what deflates the book? The sheer 500 pages of unlikable, mechanical characters, choppy, unnatural and clichéd dialogue, and Shaw’s uneven tone. It is a promising concept to combine elements of fantasy and mythology with an environmental apocalypse, but Shaw never seems to find a happy accord. There is more. The character’s backstories and internal conflicts are so frequently revisited to the point of being tedious, Shaw’s descriptions of the environment and forest only work a third of the time (when they do, they are truly beautiful. When they do not, they are horribly awkward and a result, cringeworthy… and a reader can only handle so many similes and metaphors calling attention to foliage anyway), and finally, the plot barely moves for 300 pages (largely in parts II and III) to the point of being a complete and utter bore. <br /><br />My main complaint, however, is how unbelievable human relationships are in this post-apocalyptic environment. Aside from two troublesome characters, everyone seems to be getting along just fine—no suspicion, no theft, little hostility, and very little murder. Like reviewers have said below, yes, the setting is very “twee.” (Near the climax, a character attempts to explain their ability to murder a human being by their background in landscape gardening.) Point being, there is not one character who reacts to this new, nightmarish world as they naturally would—this is not a situation where instinct has you adoring nature, befriending baby foxes, or making tribal headdresses near the top of a ruined lighthouse, watching the sunset. To Shaw’s characters, it's as if “The Trees” brought nothing more to the British Isles than a family-friendly camping ground.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17130329509351711384" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1643954172" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1643954172" />
    <a title="Sheila" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6810645-sheila"><img alt="Sheila" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1460570342p2/6810645.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1643954172?book_show_action=true">May 20, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sheila" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sheila" href="/user/show/6810645-sheila">Sheila</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/29483633-the-trees">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6810645-sheila?shelf=read-in-2016">read-in-2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6810645-sheila?shelf=netgalley">netgalley</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1643954172" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12546824101939357662">Rating this book is hard. I liked most if it, so should I give it 3 stars? I hated the ending, so should I give it 1 star? Settled on 2.5: somewhere between "I liked it" and "It was okay." (Warning: graphic violence toward animals in this book.)<br/><br/>First off, this book is gorgeous. The cover art is beautiful, and there's a little line sketch to open each chapter as well. Lovely design.<br/><br/>As far as apocalypse novels go, this was a very gentle end of the world. No one seems particularly upset (maybe it's</span>
  <span id="freeText12546824101939357662" style="display:none">Rating this book is hard. I liked most if it, so should I give it 3 stars? I hated the ending, so should I give it 1 star? Settled on 2.5: somewhere between "I liked it" and "It was okay." (Warning: graphic violence toward animals in this book.)<br /><br />First off, this book is gorgeous. The cover art is beautiful, and there's a little line sketch to open each chapter as well. Lovely design.<br /><br />As far as apocalypse novels go, this was a very gentle end of the world. No one seems particularly upset (maybe it's that English stiff upper lip?), and if you're looking for details about how the survivors prepared food, how they washed, how women dealt with menstruation, that sort of thing--you won't find it here. These details are all glossed over. <br /><br />The writing style was very simple, with stiff-sounding dialogue. However, there are themes explored here which I appreciated (what is evil? Does nature care? The cruelty of nature, etc.).<br /><br />I don't want to give too much away, but this book verges on being twee. There are some fantastical elements that I can see some readers hating. Is this book fantasy? Horror? Dystopian novel? I don't know.<br /><br />Finally, though I appreciate the character growth that happens in this book, I thought the ending was too abrupt. IMO it would have made more sense if: <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Hannah was the one transformed <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. I don't really understand Adrien's change, I guess. It seems to come out of nowhere.<br /><br />I received this review copy from the publisher on NetGalley. Thanks for the opportunity to read and review; I appreciate it! </span>
  <a data-text-id="12546824101939357662" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
